    <title>Frontiers | The Genome Sequence of the Wild Tomato Solanum pimpinellifolium Provides Insights Into Salinity Tolerance | Plant Science</title>

    <link rel="shortcut icon" href="https://3718aeafc638f96f5bd6-d4a9ca15fc46ba40e71f94dec0aad28c.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/favicon_16x16.ico" type="image/x-icon" />

    <meta property="og:title" name="Title" content="The Genome Sequence of the Wild Tomato Solanum pimpinellifolium Provides Insights Into Salinity Tolerance" />
    <meta property="og:description" name="Description" content="Solanum pimpinellifolium, a wild relative of cultivated tomato, offers a wealth of breeding potential for desirable traits such as tolerance to abiotic and biotic stresses. Here, we report the genome assembly and annotation of S. pimpinellifolium ‘LA0480.’ Moreover, we present phenotypic data from one field experiment that demonstrate a greater salinity tolerance for fruit- and yield-related traits in S. pimpinellifolium compared with cultivated tomato. The ‘LA0480’ genome assembly size (811 Mb) and the number of annotated genes (25,970) are within the range observed for other sequenced tomato species. We developed and utilized the Dragon Eukaryotic Analyses Platform (DEAP) to functionally annotate the ‘LA0480’ protein-coding genes. Additionally, we used DEAP to compare protein function between S. pimpinellifolium and cultivated tomato. Our data suggest enrichment in genes involved in biotic and abiotic stress responses. To understand the genomic basis for these differences in S. pimpinellifolium and S. lycopersicum, we analyzed 15 genes that have previously been shown to mediate salinity tolerance in plants. We show that S. pimpinellifolium has a higher copy number of the inositol-3-phosphate synthase and phosphatase genes, which are both key enzymes in the production of inositol and its derivatives. Moreover, our analysis indicates that changes occurring in the inositol phosphate pathway may contribute to the observed higher salinity tolerance in ‘LA0480.’ Altogether, ou..." />

        <meta name="citation_title" content="The Genome Sequence of the Wild Tomato Solanum pimpinellifolium Provides Insights Into Salinity Tolerance" />
        <meta name="citation_keywords" content="Wild tomato; Solanum pimpinellifolium; genome analysis; salinity tolerance; inositol 3-phosphate synthase" />
        <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Solanum pimpinellifolium, a wild relative of cultivated tomato, offers a wealth of breeding potential for desirable traits such as tolerance to abiotic and biotic stresses. Here, we report the genome assembly and annotation of S. pimpinellifolium ‘LA0480’. Moreover, we present phenotypic data from one field experiment that demonstrate a greater salinity tolerance for fruit- and yield-related traits in S. pimpinellifolium compared with cultivated tomato. The ‘LA0480’ genome assembly size (811 Mb) and the number of annotated genes (25,970) are within the range observed for other sequenced tomato species. We developed and utilized the Dragon Eukaryotic Analyses Platform (DEAP) to functionally annotate the ‘LA0480’ protein-coding genes. Additionally, we used DEAP to compare protein function between S. pimpinellifolium and cultivated tomato. Our data suggest enrichment in genes involved in biotic and abiotic stress responses. To understand the genomic basis for these differences in S. pimpinellifolium and S. lycopersicum, we analyzed 15 genes that have previously been shown to mediate salinity tolerance in plants. We show that S. pimpinellifolium has a higher copy number of the inositol-3-phosphate synthase and phosphatase genes, which are both key enzymes in the production of inositol and its derivatives. Moreover, our analysis indicates that changes occurring in the inositol phosphate pathway may contribute to the observed higher salinity tolerance in ‘LA0480’. Altogether, our work provides essential resources to understand and unlock the genetic and breeding potential of S. pimpinellifolium, and to discover the genomic basis underlying its environmental robustness." />
        <meta name="description" content="Solanum pimpinellifolium, a wild relative of cultivated tomato, offers a wealth of breeding potential for desirable traits such as tolerance to abiotic and biotic stresses. Here, we report the genome assembly and annotation of S. pimpinellifolium ‘LA0480’. Moreover, we present phenotypic data from one field experiment that demonstrate a greater salinity tolerance for fruit- and yield-related traits in S. pimpinellifolium compared with cultivated tomato. The ‘LA0480’ genome assembly size (811 Mb) and the number of annotated genes (25,970) are within the range observed for other sequenced tomato species. We developed and utilized the Dragon Eukaryotic Analyses Platform (DEAP) to functionally annotate the ‘LA0480’ protein-coding genes. Additionally, we used DEAP to compare protein function between S. pimpinellifolium and cultivated tomato. Our data suggest enrichment in genes involved in biotic and abiotic stress responses. To understand the genomic basis for these differences in S. pimpinellifolium and S. lycopersicum, we analyzed 15 genes that have previously been shown to mediate salinity tolerance in plants. We show that S. pimpinellifolium has a higher copy number of the inositol-3-phosphate synthase and phosphatase genes, which are both key enzymes in the production of inositol and its derivatives. Moreover, our analysis indicates that changes occurring in the inositol phosphate pathway may contribute to the observed higher salinity tolerance in ‘LA0480’. Altogether, our work provides essential resources to understand and unlock the genetic and breeding potential of S. pimpinellifolium, and to discover the genomic basis underlying its environmental robustness." />

        <ul class="share-media clearfix" style="padding: 0;">
            <li class="social_block">
                <div class="atButton">
                    <a class="addthis_button_facebook at300b" addthis:title="The Genome Sequence of the Wild Tomato Solanum pimpinellifolium Provides Insights Into Salinity Tolerance" addthis:description="Solanum pimpinellifolium, a wild relative of cultivated tomato, offers a wealth of breeding potential for desirable traits such as tolerance to abiotic and biotic stresses. Here, we report the genome assembly and annotation of S. pimpinellifolium ‘LA0480.’ Moreover, we present phenotypic data from one field experiment that demonstrate a greater salinity tolerance for fruit- and yield-related traits in S. pimpinellifolium compared with cultivated tomato. The ‘LA0480’ genome assembly size (811 Mb) and the number of annotated genes (25,970) are within the range observed for other sequenced tomato species. We developed and utilized the Dragon Eukaryotic Analyses Platform (DEAP) to functionally annotate the ‘LA0480’ protein-coding genes. Additionally, we used DEAP to compare protein function between S. pimpinellifolium and cultivated tomato. Our data suggest enrichment in genes involved in biotic and abiotic stress responses. To understand the genomic basis for these differences in S. pimpinellifolium and S. lycopersicum, we analyzed 15 genes that have previously been shown to mediate salinity tolerance in plants. We show that S. pimpinellifolium has a higher copy number of the inositol-3-phosphate synthase and phosphatase genes, which are both key enzymes in the production of inositol and its derivatives. Moreover, our analysis indicates that changes occurring in the inositol phosphate pathway may contribute to the observed higher salinity tolerance in ‘LA0480.’ Altogether, ou..." addthis:url="https://www.frontiersin.org/article/10.3389/fpls.2018.01402" title="Facebook"></a>

                        <h2>
                            Original Research ARTICLE

                <div class="header-bar-three">
                    Front. Plant Sci., 04 October 2018

Michell</a><sup>5&#x2021;</sup>, <a href="http://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/536684" class="user-id-536684"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/536684/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>Salim Al-Babili</a><sup>2</sup>, <a href="http://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/571539" class="user-id-571539"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/571539/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>Yung Shwen Ho</a><sup>2</sup>, <a href="http://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/13636" class="user-id-13636"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/13636/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>Mark Tester</a><sup>2</sup>, <a href="http://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/38303" class="user-id-38303"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/38303/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>Vladimir B. Bajic</a><sup>1,3*</sup> and <a href="http://www.frontiersin.org/people/u/377397" class="user-id-377397"><img class="pr5" src="https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/377397/24" onerror="this.src='https://f96a1a95aaa960e01625-a34624e694c43cdf8b40aa048a644ca4.ssl.cf2.rackcdn.com/Design/Images/newprofile_default_profileimage_new.jpg'" alt=""/>S&#x00F3;nia Negr&#x00E3;o</a><sup>2*&#x2021;</sup></div>
<ul class="notes">
<li><span><sup>1</sup></span>Computational Bioscience Research Center, King Abdullah University of Science and Technology, Thuwal, Saudi Arabia</li>
<li><span><sup>2</sup></span>Division of Biological and Environmental Sciences and Engineering, The Bioactives Lab, King Abdullah University of Science and Technology, Thuwal, Saudi Arabia</li>
<li><span><sup>3</sup></span>Division of Computer, Electrical and Mathematical Science and Engineering Division, King Abdullah University of Science and Technology, Thuwal, Saudi Arabia</li>
<li><span><sup>4</sup></span>International Center for Biosaline Agriculture, Dubai, United Arab Emirates</li>
<li><span><sup>5</sup></span>Red Sea Research Center, Biological and Environmental Sciences and Engineering Division, King Abdullah University of Science and Technology, Thuwal, Saudi Arabia</li>
</ul>
<p class="mb0"><i>Solanum pimpinellifolium</i>, a wild relative of cultivated tomato, offers a wealth of breeding potential for desirable traits such as tolerance to abiotic and biotic stresses. Here, we report the genome assembly and annotation of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> &#x2018;LA0480.&#x2019; Moreover, we present phenotypic data from one field experiment that demonstrate a greater salinity tolerance for fruit- and yield-related traits in <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> compared with cultivated tomato. The &#x2018;LA0480&#x2019; genome assembly size (811 Mb) and the number of annotated genes (25,970) are within the range observed for other sequenced tomato species. We developed and utilized the Dragon Eukaryotic Analyses Platform (DEAP) to functionally annotate the &#x2018;LA0480&#x2019; protein-coding genes. Additionally, we used DEAP to compare protein function between <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> and cultivated tomato. Our data suggest enrichment in genes involved in biotic and abiotic stress responses. To understand the genomic basis for these differences in <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, we analyzed 15 genes that have previously been shown to mediate salinity tolerance in plants. We show that <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> has a higher copy number of the inositol-3-phosphate synthase and phosphatase genes, which are both key enzymes in the production of inositol and its derivatives. Moreover, our analysis indicates that changes occurring in the inositol phosphate pathway may contribute to the observed higher salinity tolerance in &#x2018;LA0480.&#x2019; Altogether, our work provides essential resources to understand and unlock the genetic and breeding potential of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i>, and to discover the genomic basis underlying its environmental robustness.</p>
<div class="clear"></div>
</div>
<div class="JournalFullText">
<a id="h2" name="h2"></a><h2>Introduction</h2>
<p class="mb15">The <i>Solanum</i> section <i>Lycopersicon</i> is an economically important clade that consists of 14 species including the cultivated tomato <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (formerly <i>Lycopersicon esculentum</i>), which is the most economically important horticultural crop (<a href="#B98">Peralta et al., 2005</a>; <a href="#B125">Spooner et al., 2005</a>). This clade also contains <i>Solanum pimpinellifolium</i>, which is the closest wild relative of the cultivated tomato (<a href="#B140">The Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>; <a href="#B141">The 100 Tomato Genome Sequencing Consortium et al., 2014</a>). <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> has a bushy growth type, small red fruits (&#x223C;1.5 cm diameter) and is facultatively autogamous (<a href="#B109">Rick et al., 1978</a>). The distribution of the species includes the dry coastal regions of Peru, Ecuador, and northern Chile (<a href="#B79">Luckwill, 1943</a>; <a href="#B148">Warnock, 1991</a>; <a href="#B99">Peralta et al., 2008</a>), where plants are frequently exposed to brackish groundwater, salt-laden mist and other harsh environmental conditions (<a href="#B108">Rick et al., 1977</a>; <a href="#B97">Peralta and Spooner, 2000</a>; <a href="#B157">Zuriaga et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#B10">Blanca et al., 2012</a>).</p>
<p class="mb15">Due to its exposure to these challenging environmental conditions over evolutionary time, <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> exhibits a phenotypic robustness that appears to have been lost in cultivated tomato during the domestication process (<a href="#B86">Miller and Tanksley, 1990</a>; <a href="#B136">Tanksley and McCouch, 1997</a>; <a href="#B7">Bai and Lindhout, 2007</a>). Thus, <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> is regarded as an important source of genes that can confer favorable stress-tolerance to cultivated tomato. For instance, breeding tomatoes with resistance to bacterial speck disease (caused by <i>Pseudomonas syringae</i>) was achieved through the introgression of the resistance gene, <i>Pto</i>, from <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> into commercial cultivars (<a href="#B100">Pitblado and Kerr, 1979</a>; <a href="#B96">Pedley and Martin, 2003</a>; <a href="#B139">Thapa et al., 2015</a>). Furthermore, horticultural traits of commercial tomato, such as fruit size, have been influenced by the introduction of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> alleles (as reviewed by <a href="#B134">Tanksley, 2004</a>; <a href="#B6">Azzi et al., 2015</a>), some of which were identified by the molecular mapping of backcross populations developed from <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> (<a href="#B135">Tanksley et al., 1996</a>). Additionally, numerous quantitative trait loci (QTLs) have been identified using <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i>, such as those for biotic stress (<a href="#B112">Salinas et al., 2013</a>; <a href="#B24">Chen et al., 2014</a>; <a href="#B146">V&#x00ED;quez-Zamora et al., 2014</a>; <a href="#B93">Ni et al., 2017</a>), abiotic stress (<a href="#B145">Villalta et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B75">Lin et al., 2010</a>), fruit quality traits (<a href="#B135">Tanksley et al., 1996</a>; <a href="#B25">Chen et al., 1999</a>; <a href="#B149">Xiao et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B23">Capel et al., 2016</a>), and other agronomic traits (<a href="#B36">Doganlar et al., 2002</a>; <a href="#B20">Cagas et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B90">Nakano et al., 2016</a>). Numerous <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> accessions have been previously characterized as having a high salinity tolerance (ST) and are promising sources of genes and alleles for improvement of ST in cultivated tomato (<a href="#B13">Bolarin et al., 1991</a>; <a href="#B33">Cuartero et al., 1992</a>; <a href="#B44">Foolad and Lin, 1997</a>; <a href="#B42">Foolad et al., 1998</a>; <a href="#B32">Cuartero and Fernandez-Munoz, 1999</a>; <a href="#B40">Foolad, 1999</a>; <a href="#B41">Foolad and Chen, 1999</a>; <a href="#B12">Bolarin et al., 2001</a>; <a href="#B43">Foolad et al., 2001</a>; <a href="#B152">Zhang et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#B145">Villalta et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B38">Estan et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#B104">Rao et al., 2013</a>, <a href="#B105">2015</a>).</p>
<p class="mb15">To drive research and to facilitate the discovery of genes that confer favorable traits, the Tomato Genome Consortium published the high-quality genome sequence of <i>S. lycopersicum cv.</i> &#x2018;Heinz 1706,&#x2019; as well as a draft sequence of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> accession &#x2018;LA1589&#x2019; (<a href="#B140">The Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>). The availability of the cultivated tomato genome has led to several important advances, such as the identification of candidate genes (CG) related to fruit development (<a href="#B155">Zhong et al., 2013</a>; <a href="#B76">Liu et al., 2016</a>), the development of single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) genotyping arrays (<a href="#B118">Sim et al., 2012a</a>,<a href="#B119">b</a>; <a href="#B146">V&#x00ED;quez-Zamora et al., 2014</a>), the design of the CRISPR-cas9 gene-editing system (<a href="#B18">Brooks et al., 2014</a>), and the identification of loci contributing to improved tomato flavor quality (<a href="#B142">Tieman et al., 2017</a>). While the draft genome sequence of <i>S. pimpinellifolium &#x2018;</i>LA1589&#x2019; has been used in several previous studies (e.g., <a href="#B61">Kevei et al., 2015</a>), the fragmented nature of the assembly (309,180 contigs), the low sequencing coverage of the genome and the limitations of the available genome annotation constrain the usefulness of this assembly. Additionally, a further three accessions of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> (LYC2798, LA1584 and LA1578) were sequenced by the 100 Tomato Genome Project (<a href="#B141">The 100 Tomato Genome Sequencing Consortium et al., 2014</a>), but genome assemblies and annotations for these accessions have not been performed. Thus, the availability of an improved genome assembly for <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> is expected to provide increased opportunities for the discovery of new genes unique to wild germplasm within the <i>Lycopersicon</i> clade.</p>
<p class="mb0">Here we report the results of a field trial that confirms the previously reported high ST of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> relative to the commercial tomato, <i>S. lycopersicum</i> &#x2018;Heinz 1706.&#x2019; We used the <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> accession &#x2018;LA0480,&#x2019; which ranked in the top 50 accessions in terms of ST out of 200 genotypes in a recent large-scale field experiment (unpublished data). To investigate the genomic basis of this ST, we used Illumina technology to sequence the genome of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> &#x2018;LA0480&#x2019; to a depth of 197x and produced a genome assembly of 811 Mb, with final scaffold N50 of 75,736 bp (Table <a href="#T1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a href="#h11">S4</a>). This assembly is a substantial improvement on the previously reported genome assembly of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> accession &#x2018;LA1589.&#x2019; We annotated 25,134 protein-coding genes (Table <a href="#T1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a href="#h11">S9</a>) within our assembly with Dragon Eukaryotic Analysis Platform (DEAP), which is a new tool for functional genome annotation and comparison and is presented here for the first time. The DEAP (pronounced DEEP) &#x2018;Annotate&#x2019; module was used to assign annotation from multiple sources including the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) database, UniProt and InterProScan. Additionally, the DEAP &#x2018;Compare&#x2019; module was used to compare genome annotations of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>. The use of multiple comparative genomics approaches led to the identification of genes that may play a role in biotic and abiotic stress tolerance of <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> &#x2018;LA0480&#x2019; and these genes represent promising candidates for future investigation. Additionally, a CG approach led to the identification of genes encoding inositol-3-phosphate synthase (I3PS), a key enzyme involved in salinity response (<a href="#B92">Nelson et al., 1998</a>), as having a higher copy number in <i>S. pimpinellifolium</i> &#x2018;LA0480&#x2019; compared with other, less salt tolerant, tomato species. Our results suggest that I3PS and the inositol pathway may play an important role in ST in &#x2018;LA0480.&#x2019;</p>
<div class="DottedLine"></div>
<div class="Imageheaders">TABLE 1</div>
<div class="FigureDesc">
<a href="https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/390082/fpls-09-01402-HTML/image_m/fpls-09-01402-t001.jpg" name="table1" target="_blank">
